Tientsin Peace Hopes 
Brighten; Control Yuan 
To Ask For Cease-Fire 


Tientsin, January 11. 
Hopes of being spared further ravages of civil war 
soared today when the four-man peace de- 
legation returned from a satisfactory initial 
interview with the Communists outside the 


city. 


delegates—all members of the City Council 
-—who crossed into Communist territory on 


Sunday afternoon to 


appeal for a cease-fire, 


came back this morning with terms and the 
Communist promise to refrain from gun-fire 


for 24 hours, 

The unshaved and weary 
councillors; who ‘were reluct- 
ant to talk when questloned 
by Reuter, did not see Gen- 
eral Lin Piao, the Communes 
ist commander, personally but 
his. chief-of-staff, who ex- 
pressed the desire to accept 
the public plea: for peace in 
principle. 


A written statement issucet by |. 


the peace emissaries snid that the 
Communists expressed admirna- 
tion for the Councillors and thetr 
endeavours in the interests of 


‘Tientsin's 2,000,000 inhabitants, 


The Reds approved the vde- 
mands of the Tientsin people for 
pence and said that they had no 
desire to destroy Tientsin. ‘The 
Communists = expressed their, 
rendiness for peace talks and 
asked the councillors to continue 
thetr good work towards this end. 

In response tv the demands for 
peace, the Communists, the state- 
ment continued, provisionally 
ngreed to oa cease-fire from 
January 10 at 6 p.m. for 24 hours, 

The delegates left Tlentsin in a 
military jeep, which’ was supplied 
hy the Tientsin garrison and tra- 
velled to the protection dyke, 
where they disembarked and 
walked on foot under a white 
flag across no-man’s land towards 
the Communist lines. j

The Kuomintang povern- 
ment in Nankinye in the lust 
days of the Gencralissimo’s 
personal regime — resembles 
‘nothing se much as the 
fabled man who jumped) on 
his horse and dashed off in 
all directions. 


Bleak, cold) Nanking has 
been covered by a fog of 
rumours produced by frantic 
consultations and acrid) dis- 
agreements The buttered 
Kuomntany is) straining in 
four direetions: — continued 
resistanee, nepoatiatians 
throuph foreign pOWETS, 
direct negotiations with the 
“Communists and flight. The 
“smart money has all been bet 
on direet negotiations, to 
begin as svan as Generalis- 
simo Chiang Kai-shek is per- 
suaded to resign, which Is 
expected morhientarily, 

The pugnacious Generalls- 
simo has been virtually the 
only mun in Nanking whose 
face is set stubbornly in the 
direction of continued resist- 
ance. Although others talk 
about it, they recognise the 
utter hopelessness of the 
Kuomintang's military situa- 
tion. In Central China, 
vuarding the Northern. ap- 
proaches and flanks of Nan- 
king, the Kuomintang has 
under two hundred thousand 
troops—many of thém sccand 
rate-- tu battle against .up- 
wards of four hundred fifty 
thousand victorious Com- 
munist troops., Best inform- 
ed military sources predict 
that the ‘Communists who 
spent the last two weeks of 
1948 9 regrouping and re- 
organisinu could, with a two- 
week olfensive, reach the 
Yangtse River which, curves 
around Nanking to the 
Narth.... Within 
weeks they could probably 
cross the river and surround 
the capital. Few of Nan- 
king's inhabitants have any 
confidence in the military 
ability of the troops which 
have straggied through to 
take up defensive positions 
on the South bank of .the 
Yangtse. 

The Kuomintang's right- 
wing leaders led by Chen Li- 
fu, . the leader of the “CC 
Clique" and called “The 
Himmler of China”, has at- 
tempted desperately to pet 
foreign intervention __to 
mediale between the Kuo- 
mintang and the Commun- 
ists. Chen Li-fu has hoped 
that foreign mediation would 
stall off the victory of the 
Communist forces for a year 
or so during which time the 
Kuomintang could consolid- 
ate its hold in South China. 
He has also made it clear. 
that he hopes’ that World! 
War III will come soon 50 
that the Kuomintang .will be 
rescued as an ally ‘of the 
anti-Soviet forces. However, 
none of. the foreign powers 
are .willing to serve as a pro- 
tective screen for the beaten 
Kuomintang, and’ Chen Li- 

al? machine: which.

By VICTORIA CHAPPELLE 


Reading an enthusiastic des- 
eription in my favourite! fashion 
magazine of the 1949 voyue 
for stoles,; L was forced to the 
conclusion that nothing is really 
safe from the dress designer 
when he is on the luok-out 
for something new.-or some. 
thing so old that at will appear 
new to contemporary eyes. 


The smart worn in the first 
decady of this century was never 
without her stole, sometimes in 
fur, quite often in ostrich feathirs, 
(She was,.of course, unawire 
that originally her dressmoker 
had borrowed tho idea from the 
long strip or band of silk worn 
by ecelestastics). 


Thon, quite suddenly, the stole 
went out of fashion and fer 40 
years everyone forgot its exist- 
ence. Now it has been revived 
and we are going to seo it in so 
many forms that, before 2950 are 
rives, designers will be compelled, 
in self- defence, to ignore it again. 


However, for the moment wo- 
men. can enjoy. wearing — this 
eminently feminine accessory, But 
I must utter a word’ of warning: 
to wear a stole properly ts an art, 
Vike wearing:a shawl. You can 
wither lontt nonchalantly graceful, 
with your two yards or so of fur 
af fabric draped round your 
shoulders”’or you can give the 
impression — that something is 
slipping and = you haven't much 
hope of retrieving it in time, 


Grandmother's Idea

tween the stole of 1910 and that 
of 1049. Today you can have it 
made to, look very much liko a

look ns though you must be warm

But for evening, of course, a

own efforts. 


At the time I knew them 
Brenda was six and Cynthia 
four. Their parents were in- 
telligent paople with very de- 
finite ideas on how to bring up 
their children. They were quity 
certain their ideas were right. 

One day the children borrowed 
thelr mother’s sewing basket tu 
vimake dolls’ clothes and that eve- 
ning after tea the mother went 
out In the garden with a torch to 
ret some firowood and found her 
best setssors lyingg on the back 
dawn, 


She went Infa the bedroom 
where the children were getting 
Treats foe bed, saying, 
you ehildren Jeft the scissors oul. 
wide?”

the deed, "Come on, 
mother persisted, “one of you 
must hnve done if, so you may 
us well tell snes Both children

Each maintained flatly that she 
hod not left the scissors outside

alowed up the next mourning 
until a confession had been made, 


After about 10 minutes, during

voices could be heard from the

tween, sobs, confessed,

FOR STEAK 


1% Ib. round steak In one 
plece, 1 cup soft breadcrumbs, 
1 tenspoon salt, % teaspoon 
pepper, 1 tablespoon chopped 
onlon, 4 cup diced’ celery, 1 
smatl turnip, 1 small carrot, 2 
sticks celery, stock or water. 
Wipe the steak. Remove the 
skin from the outside und spread’ 
flat on a bonrd. Mix together 
the breadcrumbs, salt, pepper, 
chopped onion, /diced celery and 
suMeient stock to bind the in- 
gredients together. Spread on 
the meat, snd roll up. Tie or 
fusten with a skewer, Melt 
sumclent dripping in the bottom 
of a saucepan to fust cover. 
. When fuming hot, add the meat 
and brown well on all sides. 
Pour away any remaining dripp- 
ing, Add suMcient water or 
stock to cover. Add turnip, 
enrrot, and celery cut into large 
dice. Lid 
very gently 24% to 3 hours, When 
the ment is cooked,. remove from 


the saucepnn and thicken with}. 


gravy with a little blended flour. 
Serve the: meat on a hot. dish, 
surrounded by the vegetables and 
the gravy in a separate sauce 
boat. -

Tokyo, January 11. 
The Government is planning 


‘| measures to cope with an esti- 


mated 1,200,000 , unemployed 
that will result.in the imple- 
mentation of General Douglas 
MacArthur’s nine-point Econo- 
mic Stabilisation Programme. 


Drastic administrative reforms 
and radionalisation of industrial 
management, following the est- 
ablishinent of a single exchange 
rate for the Yer, will be responst- 
ble for aclarge’ percentage of this 
figure. 

Accorting to the Labour Minis- 
try figures, approximately 600,000 
Government workers — will lose 
their jobs us a result of the pro- 
jected adminictrative reform. 

Another extimated 600,000 work- 
exa In private Industries will be- 
come wnemployed when the  In- 
dustrial setup is reformed to give 
priority to exports. 

The Jatest statistics put the 
number of the present unemployed 
at 670,000, This would bring the 
total to n little under two million, 

The Governmont plans to sup-

MANNERS 


Bad manners are always ob- 
Jectionable. We can excuse the 
ignorant or’ the poor who know 
ho better, But there are many 
who take a certain amount at 
pride ‘in being rude, and thelr 
faults cunnot be overlooked. 

‘They think -that to be rudo lp 
a sign of independence and man- 
lness. Others say that politencss 
{s a form of insincerity, and hold 
that 11 is ketter to Ignore a per= 
son than to pretend ‘that-“I hope 
that you are well." That Is not 
right. Even should one dislike 
somecne, it is not necessary to lot 
him cr her know the unplensent 
truth, . u 

Good manners may be super- 
ficial but they are ns necessary 
to the continuance of society “as 
oil is to the lamp. And with 
people who have a natural, kind 
heart, politeness is neither in+ 
sincere nor artificial, 

The cysence of good manners is 
consideration’ for the feclings pf 
others, . : 

We are'not’ expected to like 
or to approve of all that we 
meet, but we can and must learn 
to be Rind to all, treating tham 
courteously at leart, 

it costs’ very tile to be polite,

Sydney Gets 
Heavy Rain 


Sydney, January 10, 

Heavy rains drenched the 
whole East coast’ of Australia 
today, assuring au good season 
for many primary products, 
ultimately producing more food 
for, Britain. 

Sydney had its heaviest rain- 
fall for six yours, up to 10 Inches 
being recorded in some quarters. 

Rain blocked the railway at 
Newcastle, New: South Wales, 
for six hours, flooded low-lying 
suburbs, washed away beaches 


the vessel's 


-and sank small harbour craft. 


More than 15,000 Sydney 
telephones were put out of or- 
der. 

Up to eight Inches of rain 
have “also fallen In’ the -drought- 
stricken menat-producing areas of 
Queensland and New South 
Wales.—Reuter,

Questions On 
Cost Committee 


Questions on the Report -of the 
Building Cost Committee, 1247-48, 
will be asked today at the Legis- 
lntive Council mecting, 

Sir M.K, Lo will ask the ques- 
tions, They are: 

With reference to the Report of 
the Buliding Costs Committee 
1917-1948 which was published 
in September 1948, will Govern- 
ment state— 

“(a) whether Government has 
necepted this Report and, if so, 

‘“(b)) what action Government 
has taken‘ to implement the re- 
commendations of thin Report and 
in particular, what Government 
has done or proposes to do in re- 
gard to the recanmendation, re- 
xarding the sand monopoly crented 
by Government?”

First Obstacle 
In Hunt Meet 


{By NIMROD) 

Sunday morning 1 
the good fortune to be 
to extract Mr, Jorrocks 
his bed, and we, set) out 
the hunt together. 

lt was oa One iurning wath oa 
nice nip in the aie w 
rived at the: meéet-—Mr, Moller's 
Stables, ‘The surroundings were 
reininiscent of the pitst, and when 
the Stantons were so generous to 
Mlcthe followers, 

The first obstacle of any cone 
sequence wis rather an nasty place,

Found Dead 


In Residence 


Mr. E.D, Stansfeld was found 
dead in bed early yesterday 
morning by his houseboy at his 
residence, 74 MacDonnell Road. 


Mr. Stansfeld recently retired 
from his post as Superintendent 
of Crown Lands and Survey. He 
had previously been in the civil 
service in Northern Rhodesi: 
and Jamaica, 

He arrived ~in the 
September, 1947, 


had 
able 
from 
for

last November. 
Mrs. Stansfeld was due to re- 
turn to the Colony next month.
